<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caspian_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oil_pipelines_in_the_Caspian_region.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="447" data-file-width="425" decoding="async" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Oil_pipelines_in_the_Caspian_region.gif/220px-Oil_pipelines_in_the_Caspian_region.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Oil_pipelines_in_the_Caspian_region.gif/330px-Oil_pipelines_in_the_Caspian_region.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3b/Oil_pipelines_in_the_Caspian_region.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oil_pipelines_in_the_Caspian_region.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Oil pipelines in the Caspian region. September 2002</div></div></div>
<p>Countries in the Caspian region, particularly <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a>, have high-value natural-resource-based economies, where the oil and gas compose more than 10 percent of their <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">GDP</a> and 40 percent of their exports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalyuzhnova_2008_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kalyuzhnova_2008-55">[55]</a></sup> All the Caspian region economies are highly dependent on this type of <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">mineral</a> wealth. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_energy_market" title="World energy market">world energy markets</a> were influenced by Azerbaijan and Kazakhstan, as they became strategically crucial in this sphere, thus attracting the largest share of <a href="/wiki/Foreign_direct_investment" title="Foreign direct investment">FDI</a> (foreign direct investment).
</p><p>All of the countries are rich in solar energy and harnessing potential, with the highest rainfall much less than the mountains of central Europe in the mountains of the west, which are also rich in <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectricity</a> sources.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> has high fossil fuel energy potential. It has reserves of 137.5 billion <a href="/wiki/Barrel_(unit)" title="Barrel (unit)">barrels</a> of <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">crude oil</a>, the fourth largest in the world, producing around four million barrels a day. Iran has an estimated 988.4 trillion cubic feet of <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>, around 16 percent of world reserves, thus key to current paradigms in global energy security.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalyuzhnova_2008_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kalyuzhnova_2008-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Russia" title="Economy of Russia">Russia's economy</a> ranks as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(nominal)" title="List of countries by GDP (nominal)">twelfth largest</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nominal_GDP" title="Nominal GDP">nominal GDP</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP)">sixth largest</a> by <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">purchasing power parity</a> in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Russia's extensive mineral and energy resources are the largest such reserves in the world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> making it the second leading <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_producer" title="Oil producer">producer of oil</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_gas_producer" title="Natural gas producer">natural gas</a> globally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Caspian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Littoral" title="Littoral">littoral</a> states join efforts to develop infrastructure, tourism and trade in the region. The first <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Economic_Forum" title="Caspian Economic Forum">Caspian Economic Forum</a> was convened on August 12, 2019, in Turkmenistan and brought together representatives of Kazakhstan, Russia, Azerbaijan, Iran and that state. It hosted several meetings of their ministers of economy and transport.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Caspian countries develop robust cooperation in the tech and digital field as part of the Caspian Digital Hub. The project helps expand data transmission capabilities in Kazakhstan as well as data transit capabilities between Asia and Europe. The project generated interest from investors from all over the world, including the UK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oil_and_gas">Oil and gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caspian_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Oil and gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jack-up-rig-in-the-caspian-sea.JPG"><img alt="Oil production using drilling platform, on the offshore of Turkmenistan" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1340" data-file-width="2080" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Jack-up-rig-in-the-caspian-sea.JPG/220px-Jack-up-rig-in-the-caspian-sea.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Jack-up-rig-in-the-caspian-sea.JPG/330px-Jack-up-rig-in-the-caspian-sea.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Jack-up-rig-in-the-caspian-sea.JPG/440px-Jack-up-rig-in-the-caspian-sea.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jack-up-rig-in-the-caspian-sea.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Drilling platform "Iran Khazar" in use at a Dragon Oil production platform in the <a href="/wiki/Hazar,_Turkmenistan" title="Hazar, Turkmenistan">Cheleken</a> field (<a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a>).</div></div></div><p>
The Caspian Sea region presently is a significant, but not major, supplier of <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">crude oil</a> to world markets, based upon estimates by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BP-Amoco" title="BP-Amoco">BP Amoco</a> and the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Energy_Information_Administration" title="Energy Information Administration">Energy Information Administration</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">U.S. Department of Energy</a>. The region output about 1.4–1.5 million <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barrels_of_oil" title="Barrels of oil">barrels</a> per day plus natural <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gas</a> liquids in 2001, 1.9% of total world output. More than a dozen countries output more than this top figure. Caspian region production has been higher, but waned during and after the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>. <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> accounts for 55% and <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a> for about 20% of the states' <a href="/wiki/Extraction_of_petroleum" title="Extraction of petroleum">oil output.</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caspian_region_oil_and_natural_gas_infrastructure.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2550" data-file-width="2700" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Caspian_region_oil_and_natural_gas_infrastructure.png/220px-Caspian_region_oil_and_natural_gas_infrastructure.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Caspian_region_oil_and_natural_gas_infrastructure.png/330px-Caspian_region_oil_and_natural_gas_infrastructure.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Caspian_region_oil_and_natural_gas_infrastructure.png/440px-Caspian_region_oil_and_natural_gas_infrastructure.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caspian_region_oil_and_natural_gas_infrastructure.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Caspian region oil and natural gas infrastructure. August 2013.</div></div></div>
<p>The world's first offshore wells and machine-drilled wells were made in Bibi-Heybat Bay, near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baku,_Azerbaijan" title="Baku, Azerbaijan">Baku, Azerbaijan</a>. In 1873, exploration and development of oil began in some of the largest fields known to exist in the world at that time on the <a href="/wiki/Absheron_Peninsula" title="Absheron Peninsula">Absheron Peninsula</a> near the villages of <a href="/wiki/Balaxan%C4%B1" title="Balaxanı">Balakhanli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sabanchi,_Yanaulsky_District,_Republic_of_Bashkortostan" title="Sabanchi, Yanaulsky District, Republic of Bashkortostan">Sabunchi</a>, Ramana, and Bibi Heybat. Total recoverable reserves were more than 500 million tons. By 1900, Baku had more than 3,000 oil wells, 2,000 of which were producing at industrial levels. By the end of the 19th century, Baku became known as the "black gold capital", and many skilled workers and specialists flocked to the city.
</p><p>By the beginning of the 20th century, Baku was the center of the international oil industry. In 1920, when the <a href="/wiki/Bolsheviks" title="Bolsheviks">Bolsheviks</a> captured <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>, all private property, including oil wells and factories, was confiscated. Rapidly the republic's oil industry came under the control of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>. By 1941, Azerbaijan was producing a record 23.5 million tons of oil per year – its Baku region output was nearly 72 percent of the Soviet Union's oil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SOCAR1_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-SOCAR1-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1994, the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Contract_of_the_Century_(Azerbaijan)" title="Contract of the Century (Azerbaijan)">Contract of the Century</a>" was signed, heralding extra-regional development of the Baku oil fields. The large <a href="/wiki/Baku%E2%80%93Tbilisi%E2%80%93Ceyhan_pipeline" title="Baku–Tbilisi–Ceyhan pipeline">Baku–Tbilisi–Ceyhan pipeline</a> conveys Azeri oil to the Turkish Mediterranean port of <a href="/wiki/Ceyhan" title="Ceyhan">Ceyhan</a> and opened in 2006.
</p><p>The <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Vladimir_Filanovsky&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Vladimir Filanovsky (page does not exist)">Vladimir Filanovsky</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://ru.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%A4%D0%B8%D0%BB%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B2%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D0%B9,_%D0%92%D0%BB%D0%B0%D0%B4%D0%B8%D0%BC%D0%B8%D1%80_%D0%AE%D1%80%D1%8C%D0%B5%D0%B2%D0%B8%D1%87" title="ru:Филановский, Владимир Юрьевич">ru</a>]</span> oil field in the Russian section of the body of water was discovered in 2005. It is reportedly the largest found in 25 years. It was announced in October 2016 that <a href="/wiki/Lukoil" title="Lukoil">Lukoil</a> would start production from it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caspian_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Baku" title="Baku">Baku</a> has the main moorings of all large vessels, such as oil tankers, in <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>. It is the largest port of the Caspian Sea. The port (and tankers) have access to the oceans along the Caspian Sea–Volga–<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Don_River_(Russia)" title="Don River (Russia)">Don</a> <a href="/wiki/Volga%E2%80%93Don_Canal" title="Volga–Don Canal">Canal</a>, and the Don–<a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Azov" title="Sea of Azov">Sea of Azov</a>. A northern alternate is the <a href="/wiki/Volga%E2%80%93Baltic_Waterway" title="Volga–Baltic Waterway">Volga–Baltic</a> (a sea which has a connection to the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> of the Atlantic, as the <a href="/wiki/White_Sea" title="White Sea">White Sea</a> does via the <a href="/wiki/White_Sea%E2%80%93Baltic_Canal" title="White Sea–Baltic Canal">White Sea-Baltic</a> canal. Baku Sea Trade Port and Caspian Shipping Company CJSC, have a big role in the sea transportation of Azerbaijan. The Caspian Sea Shipping Company CJSC has two fleets plus shipyards. Its transport fleet has 51 vessels: 20 tankers, 13 ferries, 15 universal dry cargo vessels, 2 Ro-Ro vessels, as well as 1 technical vessel and 1 floating workshop. Its specialized fleet has 210 vessels: 20 cranes, 25 towing and supplying vehicles, 26 passenger, two pipe-laying, six fire-fighting, seven engineering-geological, two diving and 88 auxiliary vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Caspian Sea Shipping Company of Azerbaijan, which acts as a liaison in the Transport Corridor Europe-Caucasus-Asia (TRACECA), simultaneously with the transportation of cargo and passengers in the Trans-Caspian direction, also performs work to fully ensure the processes of oil and gas production at sea. In the 19th century, the sharp increase in oil production in <a href="/wiki/Baku" title="Baku">Baku</a> gave a huge impetus to the development of shipping in the Caspian Sea, and as a result, there was a need to create fundamentally new floating facilities for the transportation of oil and oil products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_issues">Political issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caspian_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Political issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many of the islands along the Azerbaijani coast retain great geopolitical and economic importance for demarcation-line oil fields relying on their national status. <a href="/wiki/Bulla_Island" title="Bulla Island">Bulla Island</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pirallah%C4%B1_Island" title="Pirallahı Island">Pirallahı Island</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nargin_(island)" title="Nargin (island)">Nargin</a>, which is still used as a former <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a> base and is the largest island in the <a href="/wiki/Baku" title="Baku">Baku</a> bay, hold oil reserves.
</p><p>The collapse of the Soviet Union allowed the market opening of the region. This led to intense investment and development by international oil companies. In 1998, <a href="/wiki/Dick_Cheney" title="Dick Cheney">Dick Cheney</a> commented that "I can't think of a time when we've had a region emerge as suddenly to become as strategically significant as the Caspian."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>A key problem to further local development is arriving at precise, agreed demarcation lines among the five <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Littoral" title="Littoral">littoral</a> states. The current disputes along Azerbaijan's maritime borders with Turkmenistan and Iran could impinge future development.
</p><p>Much controversy currently exists over the proposed <a href="/wiki/Trans-Caspian_Oil_Transport_System" title="Trans-Caspian Oil Transport System">Trans-Caspian oil and gas pipelines</a>. These projects would allow Western markets easier access to Kazakh oil and, potentially, Uzbek and Turkmen gas as well. Russia officially opposes the project on environmental grounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scuttle_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-scuttle-66">[66]</a></sup> However, analysts note that the pipelines would bypass Russia completely, thereby denying the country valuable transit fees, as well as destroying its current <a href="/wiki/Monopoly" title="Monopoly">monopoly</a> on westward-bound <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a> exports from the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scuttle_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-scuttle-66">[66]</a></sup> Recently, both Kazakhstan and Turkmenistan have expressed their support for the Trans-Caspian Pipeline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>U.S. <a href="/wiki/Diplomatic_cable" title="Diplomatic cable">diplomatic cables</a> disclosed by <a href="/wiki/WikiLeaks" title="WikiLeaks">WikiLeaks</a> revealed that <a href="/wiki/BP" title="BP">BP</a> covered up a gas leak and <a href="/wiki/Blowout_(well_drilling)" title="Blowout (well drilling)">blowout</a> incident in September 2008 at an operating gas field in the Azeri-Chirag-Guneshi area of the Azerbaijan Caspian Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Territorial_status">Territorial status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caspian_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Territorial status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iran_southern_caspian_energy_prospects_2004.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1134" data-file-width="1052" decoding="async" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Iran_southern_caspian_energy_prospects_2004.jpg/220px-Iran_southern_caspian_energy_prospects_2004.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Iran_southern_caspian_energy_prospects_2004.jpg/330px-Iran_southern_caspian_energy_prospects_2004.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Iran_southern_caspian_energy_prospects_2004.jpg/440px-Iran_southern_caspian_energy_prospects_2004.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iran_southern_caspian_energy_prospects_2004.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Southern Caspian Energy Prospects (portion of Iran). Country Profile 2004.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baku_Bulvar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Baku_Bulvar.jpg/220px-Baku_Bulvar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Baku_Bulvar.jpg/330px-Baku_Bulvar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Baku_Bulvar.jpg/440px-Baku_Bulvar.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baku_Bulvar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Caspian Sea, Azerbaijan</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coastline">Coastline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caspian_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Coastline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Five states are located along about 4,800 km (3,000 mi) of Caspian coastline. The length of the coastline of these countries:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>Kazakhstan - 1,422 km (884 mi)</li>
<li>Turkmenistan - 1,035 km (643 mi)</li>
<li>Azerbaijan - 813 km (505 mi)</li>
<li>Russia - 747 km (464 mi)</li>
<li>Iran - 728 km (452 mi)</li></ol>
<p>Five states are located along about 6,380 km (3,960 mi) of Caspian coastline. The length of the coastline of these countries:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>Kazakhstan - 2,320 km (1,440 mi)</li>
<li>Turkmenistan - 1,200 km (750 mi)</li>
<li>Azerbaijan - 955 km (593 mi)</li>
<li>Iran - 900 km (560 mi)</li>
<li>Russia - 695 km (432 mi)</li></ol>
<p>Five states are located along about 6,500 km (4,000 mi) of Caspian coastline. The length of the coastline of these countries:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>Kazakhstan - 1,900 km (1,200 mi)</li>
<li>Turkmenistan - 1,768 km (1,099 mi)</li>
<li>Azerbaijan - 1,355 km (842 mi)</li>
<li>Russia - 820 km (510 mi)</li>
<li>Iran - 657 km (408 mi)</li></ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Facts_and_figures">Facts and figures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caspian_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Facts and figures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ol><li>Surface area: 143,244 sq mi (371,000 km<sup>2</sup>)</li>
<li>Maximum depth: 3,363 feet (1,025 m)</li>
<li>Average depth: 692 feet (211 m)</li>
<li>Length: 640 miles (1,030 km)</li>
<li>Maximum width: 270 miles (430 km)</li>
<li>Minimum width: 124 miles (200 km)</li>
<li>Coastline area: 4,237 miles (6,819 km)</li>
<li>Water volume: 18,761 cu mi (78,200 km<sup>3</sup>)</li>
<li>Altitude: 72 feet (22 m) below sea level. The Caspian Depression, a flat, lowland region encompassing the northern area of the Caspian Sea, is one of the lowest points on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>The Caspian Sea is the world's largest salt lake. Its length from north to south is 1,174 km (729 mi); its average width is 326 km (203 mi). It covers, net of an eastern very saline lagoon, 375,000 km<sup>2</sup> (145,000 sq mi). Its depth reaches 788 m (2,585 ft) near the centre, and in the southern part, 1,025 m (3,363 ft). It has no outlet so the surface level of water fluctuates a little; it averages about 25 m (82 ft) below sea level as of the year 2010. The Former Soviet Union portion covers 322,000 km<sup>2</sup> (124,000 sq mi), including the shelf zone. In deep sea fishing, diving and well-tapping the extent to a depth less than 200 m (660 ft) is 240,000 km<sup>2</sup> (93,000 sq mi).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Negotiations">Negotiations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caspian_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Negotiations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2000, negotiations as to the <a href="/wiki/Demarcation_line" title="Demarcation line">demarcation</a> of the sea had been going on for nearly a decade among all the states bordering it. Whether it was by law a sea, a lake, or an agreed hybrid, the decision would set the demarcation rules and was heavily debated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Access to mineral resources (<a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">oil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>), access for fishing, and access to <a href="/wiki/International_waters" title="International waters">international waters</a> (through Russia's <a href="/wiki/Volga" title="Volga">Volga</a> river and the canals connecting it to the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a>) all rest on the negotiations' outcome. Access to the Volga is key for market efficiency and economic diversity of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Landlocked" title="Landlocked">landlocked</a> states of Azerbaijan, Kazakhstan, and Turkmenistan. This concerns Russia as more traffic seeks to use — and at some points congest — its inland <a href="/wiki/Waterway" title="Waterway">waterways</a>. If the body of water is, by law, a sea, many precedents and international treaties oblige free access to foreign vessels. If it is a lake there are no such obligations.
</p><p>Resolving and improving some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environment_(biophysical)" title="Environment (biophysical)">environmental</a> issues properly rests on the status and <a href="/wiki/Border" title="Border">borders</a> issue.
</p><p>All five Caspian littoral states maintain naval forces on the sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to a treaty signed between Iran and the Soviet Union, the sea is technically a lake and was divided into two sectors (Iranian and Soviet), but the resources (then mainly fish) were commonly shared. The line between the two sectors was considered an international border in a common lake, like <a href="/wiki/Lake_Albert_(Africa)" title="Lake Albert (Africa)">Lake Albert</a>. The Soviet sector was sub-divided into the four littoral republics' administrative sectors.
</p><p>Russia, Kazakhstan, and Azerbaijan have bilateral agreements with each other based on <a href="/wiki/Median" title="Median">median</a> lines. Because of their use by the three nations, median lines seem to be the most likely method of delineating territory in future agreements. However, Iran insists on a single, multilateral agreement between the five nations (aiming for a one-fifth share). Azerbaijan is at odds with Iran over some of the sea's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_field" title="Oil field">oil fields</a>. Occasionally, Iranian patrol boats have fired at vessels sent by Azerbaijan for exploration into the disputed region. There are similar tensions between Azerbaijan and Turkmenistan (the latter claims that the former has pumped more oil than agreed from a field, recognized by both parties as shared).
</p><p>The Caspian littoral states' meeting in 2007 signed an accord that only allows littoral-state flag-bearing ships to enter the sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (September 2015)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Negotiations among the five states ebbed and flowed, from about 1990 to 2018. Progress was notable in the fourth Caspian Summit held in <a href="/wiki/Astrakhan" title="Astrakhan">Astrakhan</a> in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Caspian_Summit">Caspian Summit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caspian_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Caspian Summit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Caspian Summit is a head of state-level meeting of the five littoral states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-summit_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-summit-78">[78]</a></sup> The fifth Caspian Summit took place on August 12, 2018, in the Kazakh port city of <a href="/wiki/Aktau" title="Aktau">Aktau</a> .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-summit_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-summit-78">[78]</a></sup> The five leaders signed the ‘Convention on the Legal Status of the Caspian Sea’.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-signed_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-signed-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Representatives of the Caspian littoral states held a meeting in the capital of Kazakhstan on September 28, 2018, as a follow-up to the Aktau Summit. The conference was hosted by the Kazakh Ministry of Investment and Development. The participants in the meeting agreed to host an investment forum for the Caspian region every two years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Convention_on_the_legal_status_of_the_Caspian_Sea">Convention on the legal status of the Caspian Sea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caspian_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Convention on the legal status of the Caspian Sea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_the_legal_status_of_the_Caspian_Sea" title="Convention on the legal status of the Caspian Sea">Convention on the legal status of the Caspian Sea</a></div>
<p>The five littoral states build consensus on legally binding governance of the Caspian Sea through Special Working Groups of a Convention on the Legal Status of the Caspian Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-convlegalstatus_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-convlegalstatus-81">[81]</a></sup> In advance of a Caspian Summit, the 51st Special Working Group took place in <a href="/wiki/Astana" title="Astana">Astana</a> in May 2018 and found consensus on multiple agreements: Agreements on cooperation in the field of transport; trade and economic cooperation; prevention of incidents on the sea; combating terrorism; fighting against organized crime; and border security cooperation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51agreement_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-51agreement-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>The convention grants jurisdiction over 24 km (15 mi) of territorial waters to each neighboring country, plus an additional 16 km (10 mi) of exclusive fishing rights on the surface, while the rest is <a href="/wiki/International_waters" title="International waters">international waters</a>. The seabed, on the other hand, remains undefined, subject to bilateral agreements between countries. Thus, the Caspian Sea is legally neither fully a sea nor a lake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>While the convention addresses caviar production, oil and gas extraction, and military uses, it does not touch on environmental issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nation-20190418_23-4"><a href="#cite_note-Nation-20190418-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crossborder_inflow">Crossborder inflow</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caspian_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Crossborder inflow">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

These are:
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caspian_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Transportation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Although the Caspian Sea is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endorheic" title="Endorheic">endorheic</a>, its main tributary, the <a href="/wiki/Volga" title="Volga">Volga</a>, is connected by important shipping canals <a href="/wiki/Volga%E2%80%93Don_Canal" title="Volga–Don Canal">with the Don River</a> (and thus the Black Sea) and <a href="/wiki/Volga%E2%80%93Baltic_Waterway" title="Volga–Baltic Waterway">with the Baltic Sea</a>, with branch canals <a href="/wiki/Northern_Dvina_Canal" title="Northern Dvina Canal">to Northern Dvina</a> and <a href="/wiki/White_Sea%E2%80%93Baltic_Canal" title="White Sea–Baltic Canal">to the White Sea</a>.
</p><p>Another Caspian tributary, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kuma_River_(Russia)" title="Kuma River (Russia)">Kuma River</a>, is connected by <a href="/wiki/Kuma%E2%80%93Manych_Canal" title="Kuma–Manych Canal">an irrigation canal</a> with the Don basin as well.
</p><p>Scheduled <a href="/wiki/Ferry" title="Ferry">ferry</a> services (including <a href="/wiki/Train_ferry" title="Train ferry">train ferries</a>) across the sea chiefly are between:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/T%C3%BCrkmenba%C5%9Fy,_Turkmenistan" title="Türkmenbaşy, Turkmenistan">Türkmenbaşy</a> in <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a>, (formerly <a href="/wiki/T%C3%BCrkmenba%C5%9Fy,_Turkmenistan" title="Türkmenbaşy, Turkmenistan">Krasnovodsk</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Baku" title="Baku">Baku</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aktau" title="Aktau">Aktau</a>, Kazakhstan and Baku.</li>
<li>Cities in Iran and Russia (chiefly for cargo.)</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canals">Canals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caspian_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Canals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As an <a href="/wiki/Endorheic_basin" title="Endorheic basin">endorheic basin</a>, the Caspian Sea basin has no natural connection with the ocean. Since the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_Period" title="Medieval Period">medieval period</a>, traders reached the Caspian via a number of <a href="/wiki/Portage" title="Portage">portages</a> that connected the Volga and its tributaries with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Don_River_(Russia)" title="Don River (Russia)">Don River</a> (which flows into the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Azov" title="Sea of Azov">Sea of Azov</a>) and various rivers that flow into the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a>. <a href="/wiki/Volga%E2%80%93Baltic_Waterway#History" title="Volga–Baltic Waterway">Primitive canals</a> connecting the Volga Basin with the Baltic were constructed as early as the early 18th century. Since then, a number of canal projects have been completed.
</p><p>The two modern canal systems that connect the Volga Basin, and hence the Caspian Sea, with the ocean are the <a href="/wiki/Volga%E2%80%93Baltic_Waterway" title="Volga–Baltic Waterway">Volga–Baltic Waterway</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Volga%E2%80%93Don_Canal" title="Volga–Don Canal">Volga–Don Canal</a>.
</p><p>The proposed <a href="/wiki/Pechora%E2%80%93Kama_Canal" title="Pechora–Kama Canal">Pechora–Kama Canal</a> was a project that was widely discussed between the 1930s and 1980s. Shipping was a secondary consideration. Its main goal was to <a href="/wiki/Northern_river_reversal" title="Northern river reversal">redirect some of the water</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pechora_River" title="Pechora River">Pechora River</a> (which flows into the Arctic Ocean) via the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kama_River" title="Kama River">Kama River</a> into the Volga. The goals were both irrigation and the stabilization of the water level in the Caspian, which was thought to be falling dangerously fast at the time. During 1971, some <a href="/wiki/Peaceful_nuclear_explosion" title="Peaceful nuclear explosion">peaceful nuclear construction experiments</a> were carried out in the region by the U.S.S.R.
</p><p>In June 2007, in order to boost his oil-rich country's access to markets, <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>'s President <a href="/wiki/Nursultan_Nazarbayev" title="Nursultan Nazarbayev">Nursultan Nazarbayev</a> proposed a 700-kilometre (435-mile) link between the Caspian Sea and the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>. It is hoped that the "<a href="/wiki/Eurasia_Canal" title="Eurasia Canal">Eurasia Canal</a>" (<a href="/wiki/Manych_Ship_Canal" title="Manych Ship Canal">Manych Ship Canal</a>) would transform landlocked Kazakhstan and other <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asian countries</a> into maritime states, enabling them to significantly increase trade volume. Although the canal would traverse Russian territory, it would benefit Kazakhstan through its Caspian Sea ports. The most likely route for the canal, the officials at the Committee on Water Resources at Kazakhstan's Agriculture Ministry say, would follow the <a href="/wiki/Kuma%E2%80%93Manych_Depression" title="Kuma–Manych Depression">Kuma–Manych Depression</a>, where currently a chain of rivers and lakes is already connected by an irrigation canal (the <a href="/wiki/Kuma%E2%80%93Manych_Canal" title="Kuma–Manych Canal">Kuma–Manych Canal</a>). Upgrading the Volga–Don Canal would be another option.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
